     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       <b>
        Spinosaurus
       </b>
      </i>
      (meaning "spine lizard") is a
      <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">
       genus
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Theropoda" title="Theropoda">
       theropod
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">
       dinosaur
      </a>
      that lived in what now is
      <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">
       North Africa
      </a>
      , during the lower
      <a href="/wiki/Albian" title="Albian">
       Albian
      </a>
      to lower
      <a href="/wiki/Cenomanian" title="Cenomanian">
       Cenomanian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faunal_stage" title="Faunal stage">
       stages
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">
       Cretaceous
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Period_(geology)" title="Period (geology)">
       period
      </a>
      , about 112 to 97
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annum" title="Annum">
       million years ago
      </a>
      . This genus was known first from
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egyptian
      </a>
      remains discovered in 1912 and described by
      <a href="/wiki/Germans" title="Germans">
       German
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">
       paleontologist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Stromer" title="Ernst Stromer">
       Ernst Stromer
      </a>
      in 1915. The original remains were destroyed in
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      , but additional material has come to light in recent years. It is unclear whether one or two species are represented in the fossils reported in the scientific literature. The best known species is
      <i>
       S. aegyptiacus
      </i>
      from Egypt, although a potential second species,
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      , has been recovered from
      <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
       Morocco
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was among the largest of all known
      <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">
       carnivorous
      </a>
      dinosaurs, possibly larger than
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">
        Tyrannosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Giganotosaurus" title="Giganotosaurus">
        Giganotosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      . Estimates published in 2005, 2007, and 2008 suggested that it was between 12.618 metres (4159ft) in length and 7 to 20.9 tonnes (7.7 to 23.0 short tons) in weight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holtz2008_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Holtz2008-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      New estimates published in 2014 and based on more complete specimens, supported the earlier research, finding that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      could reach lengths greater than 15m (49ft).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibrahim_et_al_2014_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ibrahim_et_al_2014-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">
       skull
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was long and narrow, similar to that of a modern
      <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">
       crocodilian
      </a>
      .
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      is known to have eaten fish, and most scientists believe that it hunted both terrestrial and aquatic prey; evidence suggests that it lived both on land and in water as a modern crocodilian does. The distinctive
      <a href="/wiki/Spinous_process" title="Spinous process">
       spines
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      , which were long extensions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">
       vertebrae
      </a>
      , grew to at least 1.65 meters (5.4ft) long and were likely to have had skin connecting them, forming a
      <a href="/wiki/Sail_(anatomy)" title="Sail (anatomy)">
       sail-like structure
      </a>
      , although some authors have suggested that the spines were covered in fat and formed a hump. Multiple functions have been put forward for this structure, including
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       thermoregulation
      </a>
      and display.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
       Description
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Largesttheropods.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="1339" height="71" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Largesttheropods.png/220px-Largesttheropods.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Largesttheropods.png/330px-Largesttheropods.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Largesttheropods.png/440px-Largesttheropods.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Largesttheropods.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Size comparison of selected giant theropod dinosaurs,
        <i>
         S. aegyptiacus
        </i>
        in red
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since its discovery,
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      has been a contender for the longest and largest theropod dinosaur.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Both
      <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_von_Huene" title="Friedrich von Huene">
       Friedrich von Huene
      </a>
      in 1926
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vH26_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vH26-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Donald_F._Glut" title="Donald F. Glut">
       Donald F. Glut
      </a>
      in 1982 listed it as among the most massive theropods in their surveys, at 15 meters (49ft) in length and upwards of 6t (5.9 long tons; 6.6 short tons) in weight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG82_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DFG82-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1988,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gregory_Paul" title="Gregory Paul">
       Gregory Paul
      </a>
      also listed it as the longest theropod at 15 meters (49ft), but gave a lower mass estimate of 4 tonnes (3.9 long tons; 4.4 short tons).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GSP88-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Dal Sasso
      <i>
       et al
      </i>
      . (2005) assumed that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Suchomimus" title="Suchomimus">
        Suchomimus
       </a>
      </i>
      had the same body proportions in relation to their skull lengths, and thereby calculated that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was 16 to 18 meters (52 to 59ft) in length and 7 to 9 tonnes (6.9 to 8.9 long tons; 7.7 to 9.9 short tons) in weight.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Dal Sasso
      <i>
       et al
      </i>
      . estimates were criticized because the skull length estimate was uncertain, and (assuming that body mass increases as the cube of body length) scaling
      <i>
       Suchomimus
      </i>
      which was 11 meters (36ft) long and 3.8 tonnes (4.2 short tons) in mass to the range of estimated lengths of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      would produce an estimated body mass of 11.7 to 16.7 tonnes (12.9 to 18.4 short tons).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1280" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg/220px-Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg/330px-Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg/440px-Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_skull_steveoc.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         S. aegyptiacus
        </i>
        head based on the 2005 Dal Sasso reconstruction
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Franois Therrien and Donald Henderson, in a 2007 paper using scaling based on skull length, challenged previous estimates of the size of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      , finding the length too great and the weight too small.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Based on estimated skull lengths of 1.5 to 1.75 meters (4.9 to 5.7ft), their estimates include a body length of 12.6 to 14.3 meters (41 to 47ft) and a body mass of 12 to 20.9 tonnes (11.8 to 20.6 long tons; 13.2 to 23.0 short tons).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_2-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The lower estimates for
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      would imply that the animal was shorter and lighter than
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Giganotosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_2-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Therrien and Henderson study has been criticized for the choice of theropods used for comparison (e.g., most of the theropods used to set the initial equations were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurid" title="Tyrannosaurid">
       tyrannosaurids
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Carnosauria" title="Carnosauria">
       carnosaurs
      </a>
      , which have a different build than spinosaurids), and for the assumption that the
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      skull could be as little as 1.5 meters (4.9ft) in length.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Improvement of the precision of size estimates for
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      requires the discovery of more complete remains as available for some other dinosaurs,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      especially the limb bones of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      which are "hitherto unknown".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Neural_spines">
       Neural spines
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg/220px-Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg/330px-Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg/440px-Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_of_Stromer%27s_Spinosaurus_Display.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Reconstruction of the holotype fossils
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Very tall
      <a href="/wiki/Spinous_process" title="Spinous process">
       neural spines
      </a>
      growing on the back
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">
       vertebrae
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      formed the basis of what is usually called the animal's "sail". The lengths of the neural spines reached over 10 times the diameters of the vertebral bodies from which they extended.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The neural spines were slightly longer front to back at the base than higher up, and were unlike the thin rods seen in the
      <a href="/wiki/Pelycosaur" title="Pelycosaur">
       pelycosaur
      </a>
      finbacks
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Edaphosaurus" title="Edaphosaurus">
        Edaphosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon">
        Dimetrodon
       </a>
      </i>
      , contrasting also with the thicker spines in the iguanodontian
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ouranosaurus" title="Ouranosaurus">
        Ouranosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      sails were unusual, although other dinosaurs, namely the
      <a href="/wiki/Ornithopod" title="Ornithopod">
       ornithopod
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ouranosaurus" title="Ouranosaurus">
        Ouranosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      , which lived a few million years earlier in the same general region as
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      , and the South American sauropod
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amargasaurus" title="Amargasaurus">
        Amargasaurus
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      might have developed similar structural adaptations of their vertebrae. The sail may be an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analogy_(biology)" title="Analogy (biology)">
       analog
      </a>
      of the sail of the
      <a href="/wiki/Permian" title="Permian">
       Permian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Synapsid" title="Synapsid">
       synapsid
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dimetrodon" title="Dimetrodon">
        Dimetrodon
       </a>
      </i>
      , which lived before the dinosaurs even appeared, produced by
      <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">
       convergent evolution
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The structure may also have been more hump-like than sail-like, as noted by Stromer in 1915 ("one might rather think of the existence of a large hump of fat [
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_Language" title="German Language">
       German
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       Fettbuckel
      </i>
      ], to which the [neural spines] gave internal support")
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and by Jack Bowman Bailey in 1997.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In support of his "buffalo-back" hypothesis, Bailey argued that in
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       Ouranosaurus
      </i>
      , and other dinosaurs with long neural spines, the spines were relatively shorter and thicker than the spines of
      <a href="/wiki/Pelycosaur" title="Pelycosaur">
       pelycosaurs
      </a>
      (which were known to have sails); instead, the dinosaurs' neural spines were similar to the neural spines of extinct hump-backed mammals such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Megacerops" title="Megacerops">
        Megacerops
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bison_latifrons" title="Bison latifrons">
        Bison latifrons
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Skull">
       Skull
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="630" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg/220px-Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg/330px-Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg/440px-Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_skull_en.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Annotated skull diagram
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The skull had a narrow snout filled with straight conical teeth that lacked serrations. There were six or seven teeth on each side of the very front of the upper jaw, in the
      <a href="/wiki/Premaxilla" title="Premaxilla">
       premaxillae
      </a>
      , and another twelve in both
      <a href="/wiki/Maxilla" title="Maxilla">
       maxillae
      </a>
      behind them. The second and third teeth on each side were noticeably larger than the rest of the teeth in the premaxilla, creating a space between them and the large teeth in the
      <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location" title="Anatomical terms of location">
       anterior
      </a>
      maxilla; large teeth in the lower jaw faced this space. The very tip of the snout holding those few large anterior teeth was expanded, and a small crest was present in front of the eyes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using the dimensions of three specimens known as MSNM V4047, UCPC-2, and BSP 1912 VIII 19, and assuming that the postorbital part of the skull of MSNM V4047 had a shape similar to the postorbital part of the skull of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Irritator" title="Irritator">
        Irritator
       </a>
      </i>
      , Dal Sasso
      <i>
       et al
      </i>
      . (2005) estimated that the skull of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was 1.75 meters (5.7ft) long.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Dal Sasso
      <i>
       et al
      </i>
      . skull length estimate was questioned because skull shapes can vary across spinosaurid species.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TH07_2-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-TH07-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A newly made study performed by scientists Andrew R. Cuff and Emily Rayfield showed that Spinosaurids like
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      had relatively poor resistance in their skulls for torsion compared to other members of this group (
      <i>
       Baryonyx
      </i>
      ) and modern alligators, thus showing Spinosaurus preyed more regularly on fish than it did on land animals, although considered predators of the former too.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cuff13_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cuff13-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
       Classification
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1680" data-file-width="3406" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg/220px-%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg/330px-%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg/440px-%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%BF%D0%B8%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B7%D0%B0%D0%B2%D1%80_-_%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%81%D1%82%D1%80%D1%83%D0%BA%D1%86%D0%B8%D1%8F.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Restoration based on the 2014 description
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      gives its name to the
      <a href="/wiki/Spinosauridae" title="Spinosauridae">
       Spinosauridae
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">
       family
      </a>
      of dinosaurs, which includes two subfamilies: Baryonychinae and Spinosaurinae. The Baryonychinae include
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Baryonyx" title="Baryonyx">
        Baryonyx
       </a>
      </i>
      from southern
      <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">
       England
      </a>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Suchomimus" title="Suchomimus">
        Suchomimus
       </a>
      </i>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">
       Niger
      </a>
      in central
      <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
       Africa
      </a>
      . The Spinosaurinae include
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Irritator" title="Irritator">
        Irritator
       </a>
      </i>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
       Brazil
      </a>
      , and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angaturama" title="Angaturama">
        Angaturama
       </a>
      </i>
      (which is probably synonymous with
      <i>
       Irritator
      </i>
      ) from Brazil.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Spinosaurinae share unserrated straight teeth that are widely spaced (e.g., 12 on one side of the maxilla), as opposed to the Baryonychinae which have serrated curved teeth that are numerous (e.g., 30 on one side of the maxilla).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HMC04-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The following
      <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">
       cladogram
      </a>
      shows a 2010 analysis of
      <a href="/wiki/Tetanurae" title="Tetanurae">
       Tetanurae
      </a>
      simplified to show only Spinosauridae from Allain
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      (2012):
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AXRK12_16-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AXRK12-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" style="font-size:100%;line-height:85%">
