     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_naming">
       Discovery and naming
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_of_species">
       Naming of species
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1129" data-file-width="3138" height="79" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg/220px-Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg/330px-Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg/440px-Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_aegyptiacus_holotype_skeletal.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Skeletal restoration showing the holotype elements
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Two species of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      have been named:
      <i>
       Spinosaurus aegyptiacus
      </i>
      (meaning "Egyptian spine lizard") and
      <i>
       Spinosaurus maroccanus
      </i>
      (meaning "Moroccan spine lizard").
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first described remains of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      were found and described in the early 20th century. In 1912, Richard Markgraf discovered a partial skeleton of a dinosaur in the
      <a href="/wiki/Bahariya_Formation" title="Bahariya Formation">
       Bahariya Formation
      </a>
      of western Egypt. In 1915, German paleontologist
      <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Stromer" title="Ernst Stromer">
       Ernst Stromer
      </a>
      published an article assigning the specimen to a new genus and species
      <i>
       Spinosaurus aegyptiacus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Fragmentary additional remains from Bahariya, including vertebrae and hindlimb bones, were designated by Stromer as "
      <i>
       Spinosaurus B
      </i>
      " in 1934.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stromer34_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-stromer34-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stromer considered them different enough to belong to another species, and this has been borne out. With the advantage of more expeditions and material, it appears that they pertain either to
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">
        Carcharodontosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal98_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal98-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or to
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sigilmassasaurus" title="Sigilmassasaurus">
        Sigilmassasaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      was originally described by
      <a href="/wiki/Dale_Russell" title="Dale Russell">
       Dale Russell
      </a>
      in 1996 as a new species based on the length of its neck vertebrae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Specifically, Russell claimed that the ratio of the length of the centrum (
      <a href="/wiki/Body_of_vertebra" title="Body of vertebra">
       body of vertebra
      </a>
      ) to the height of the posterior articular facet was 1.1 in
      <i>
       S. aegyptiacus
      </i>
      and 1.5 in
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later authors have been split on this topic. Some authors note that the length of the vertebrae can vary from individual to individual, that the holotype specimen was destroyed and thus cannot be compared directly with the
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      specimen, and that it is unknown which cervical vertebrae the
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      specimens represent. Therefore, though some have retained the species as valid without much comment,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HMC04_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-HMC04-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hasegawa2010_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hasegawa2010-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      most researchers regard
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      as a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nomen_dubium" title="Nomen dubium">
        nomen dubium
       </a>
      </i>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BufOua02_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BufOua02-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rauhut03_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rauhut03-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or as a junior synonym of
      <i>
       S. aegyptiacus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal98_21-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal98-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Specimens">
       Specimens
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Six main partial specimens of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      have been described.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1746" data-file-width="2594" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg/220px-Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg/330px-Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg/440px-Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_holotype.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Plate I in Stromer (1915) showing
        <i>
         S. aegyptiacus
        </i>
        holotype elements
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       BSP 1912 VIII 19
      </b>
      , described by Stromer in 1915 from the
      <a href="/wiki/Bahariya_Formation" title="Bahariya Formation">
       Bahariya Formation
      </a>
      , was the
      <a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">
       holotype
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The material consisted of the following items, most of which were incomplete: right and left
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dentary" title="Dentary">
       dentaries
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Splenial" title="Splenial">
       splenials
      </a>
      from the lower jaw measuring 75 centimeters (30in) long; a straight piece of the left
      <a href="/wiki/Maxilla" title="Maxilla">
       maxilla
      </a>
      that was described but not drawn; 20 teeth; 2
      <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">
       cervical
      </a>
      vertebrae; 7 dorsal (trunk) vertebrae; 3 sacral vertebrae; 1
      <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">
       caudal
      </a>
      vertebra; 4 thoracic ribs; and
      <a href="/wiki/Gastralium" title="Gastralium">
       gastralia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Of the nine neural spines whose heights are given, the longest ("i," associated with a dorsal vertebra) was 1.65 meters (5.4ft) in length.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Stromer claimed that the specimen was from the early Cenomanian, approximately 97 million years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This specimen was destroyed in
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      , specifically "during the night of 24/25 April 1944 in a British bombing raid of Munich" that severely damaged the building housing the
      <a href="/wiki/Pal%C3%A4ontologisches_Museum_M%C3%BCnchen" title="Palontologisches Museum Mnchen">
       Palontologisches Museum Mnchen
      </a>
      (Bavarian State Collection of Paleontology).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, detailed drawings and descriptions of the specimen remain. Stromer's son donated Stromer's archives to the Palontologische Staatssammlung Mnchen in 1995, and Smith
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      analyzed two photographs of the
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      holotype specimen BSP 1912 VIII 19 discovered in the archives in 2000.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the basis of a photograph of the lower jaw and a photograph of the entire specimen as mounted, Smith concluded that Stromer's original 1915 drawings were slightly inaccurate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2003, Oliver Rauhut suggested that Stromer's
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">
       holotype
      </a>
      was a
      <a href="/wiki/Chimera_(paleontology)" title="Chimera (paleontology)">
       chimera
      </a>
      , composed of vertebrae and neural spines from a
      <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosauridae" title="Carcharodontosauridae">
       carcharodontosaurid
      </a>
      similar to
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Acrocanthosaurus" title="Acrocanthosaurus">
        Acrocanthosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dentary" title="Dentary">
       dentary
      </a>
      from
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Baryonyx" title="Baryonyx">
        Baryonyx
       </a>
      </i>
      or
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Suchomimus" title="Suchomimus">
        Suchomimus
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rauhut03_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Rauhut03-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This analysis was rejected in at least one subsequent paper.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       NMC 50791
      </b>
      , held by the
      <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Museum_of_Nature" title="Canadian Museum of Nature">
       Canadian Museum of Nature
      </a>
      , is a mid-cervical vertebra which is 19.5 centimeters (7.7in) long from the
      <a href="/wiki/Kem_Kem_Beds" title="Kem Kem Beds">
       Kem Kem Beds
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
       Morocco
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is the holotype of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus maroccanus
      </i>
      as described by Russell in 1996.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other specimens referred to
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      in the same paper were two other mid-cervical vertebrae (NMC 41768 and NMC 50790), an anterior dentary fragment (NMC 50832), a mid-dentary fragment (NMC 50833), and an anterior dorsal
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebral_arch" title="Vertebral arch">
       neural arch
      </a>
      (NMC 50813).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Russell stated that "only general locality information could be provided" for the specimen, and therefore it could be dated only "possibly" to the Albian.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAR96_18-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-DAR96-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="2745" height="49" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg/220px-Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg/330px-Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg/440px-Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_marocannus.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Specimen MNHN SAM 124 of
        <i>
         S. maroccanus
        </i>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9um_national_d%27Histoire_naturelle" title="Musum national d'Histoire naturelle">
         Musum National d'Histoire Naturelle
        </a>
        , Paris
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       MNHN SAM 124
      </b>
      , housed at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9um_National_d%27Histoire_Naturelle" title="Musum National d'Histoire Naturelle">
       Musum National d'Histoire Naturelle
      </a>
      , is a snout (consisting of partial premaxillae, partial maxillae,
      <a href="/wiki/Vomer" title="Vomer">
       vomers
      </a>
      , and a dentary fragment).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taquet1998_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Taquet1998-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Described by Taquet and Russell in 1998, the specimen is 13.4to 13.6 centimeters (5.35.4in) in width; no length was stated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taquet1998_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Taquet1998-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The specimen was located in Algeria, and "is of Albian age."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taquet1998_26-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Taquet1998-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Taquet and Russell believed that this specimen along with a premaxilla fragment (SAM 125), two cervical vertebrae (SAM 126-127), and a dorsal neural arch (SAM 128), belonged to
      <i>
       S. maroccanus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taquet1998_26-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Taquet1998-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       BM231
      </b>
      (in the collection of the Office National des Mines, Tunis) was described by Buffetaut and Ouaja in 2002.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BufOua02_24-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BufOua02-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It consists of a partial anterior dentary 11.5 centimetres (4.53in) in length from an early Albian
      <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">
       stratum
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Chenini_Formation" title="Chenini Formation">
       Chenini Formation
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">
       Tunisia
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BufOua02_24-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-BufOua02-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The dentary fragment, which included four
      <a href="/wiki/Dental_alveolus" title="Dental alveolus">
       alveoli
      </a>
      and two partial teeth, was "extremely similar" to existing material of
      <i>
       S. aegyptiacus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BufOua02_24-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-BufOua02-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <b>
       UCPC-2
      </b>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">
       University of Chicago
      </a>
      Paleontological Collection consists mainly of two narrow connected
      <a href="/wiki/Nasal_bone" title="Nasal bone">
       nasals
      </a>
      with a "fluted crest" from the region between the eyes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The specimen, which is 18.0 centimetres (7.09in) long, was located in an early Cenomanian part of the Moroccan Kem Kem Beds in 1996 and described in the scientific literature in 2005 by
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cristiano_Dal_Sasso&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cristiano Dal Sasso (page does not exist)">
       Cristiano Dal Sasso
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_Civico_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Milan" title="Museo Civico di Storia Naturale di Milan">
       Civic Natural History Museum
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">
       Milan
      </a>
      and colleagues.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinocombo.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1964" data-file-width="4906" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Spinocombo.jpg/220px-Spinocombo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Spinocombo.jpg/330px-Spinocombo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Spinocombo.jpg/440px-Spinocombo.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinocombo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Specimen MSNM V4047 of
        <i>
         S. aegyptiacus
        </i>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Museo_Civico_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Milano" title="Museo Civico di Storia Naturale di Milano">
         Civic Natural History Museum
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">
         Milan
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       MSNM V4047
      </b>
      (in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_Civico_di_Storia_Naturale_di_Milan" title="Museo Civico di Storia Naturale di Milan">
       Museo di Storia Naturale di Milano
      </a>
      ), described by Dal Sasso
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      in 2005, consists of a snout (premaxillae, partial maxillae, and partial nasals) 98.8 centimetres (38.9in) long from the Kem Kem Beds.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Like UCPC-2, it is thought to have come from the early Cenomanian.
     </p>
     <p>
      Other known specimens consist mainly of very fragmentary remains and scattered teeth. These include:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Possible material belonging to
       <i>
        Spinosaurus
       </i>
       has been reported from the
       <a href="/wiki/Turkana_Grits" title="Turkana Grits">
        Turkana Grits
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">
        Kenya
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weishampel04_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Weishampel04-27">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         27
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       A 1986 paper described prismatic structures in
       <a href="/wiki/Tooth_enamel" title="Tooth enamel">
        tooth enamel
       </a>
       from two
       <i>
        Spinosaurus
       </i>
       teeth from Tunisia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         28
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Buffetaut (1989, 1992) referred three specimens from the Institut und Museum fr Geologie und Palontologie of the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_G%C3%B6ttingen" title="University of Gttingen">
        University of Gttingen
       </a>
       in Germany to
       <i>
        Spinosaurus
       </i>
       : a right maxilla fragment IMGP 969-1, a jaw fragment IMGP 969-2, and a tooth IMGP 969-3.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buffetaut89_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buffetaut89-29">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         29
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buffetaut92_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buffetaut92-30">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         30
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       These had been found in a Lower Cenomanian or Upper Albian deposit in southeastern Morocco in 1971.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buffetaut89_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buffetaut89-29">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         29
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5376" data-file-width="6048" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg/220px-Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg/330px-Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg/440px-Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_Crane.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Skull of a privately owned reconstructed skeleton of a subadult
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Kellner and Mader (1997) described two unserrated spinosaurid teeth from Morocco (LINHM 001 and 002) that were "highly similar" to the teeth of the
       <i>
        S. aegyptiacus
       </i>
       holotype.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         31
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Teeth from the Chenini Formation in Tunisia which are "narrow, somewhat rounded in cross-section, and lack the anterior and posterior serrated edges characteristic of theropods and basal
       <a href="/wiki/Archosaur" title="Archosaur">
        archosaurs
       </a>
       " were assigned to
       <i>
        Spinosaurus
       </i>
       in 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         32
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Teeth from the
       <a href="/wiki/Echkar_Formation" title="Echkar Formation">
        Echkar Formation
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">
        Niger
       </a>
       were "tentatively" referred to
       <i>
        Spinosaurus
       </i>
       in 2007.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brusatte.26sereno2007_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-brusatte.26sereno2007-33">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         33
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       A partial tooth 8cm long purchased at a fossil trade show, reportedly from the Kem Kem Bed of Morocco and attributed to
       <i>
        Spinosaurus maroccanus
       </i>
       , showed 15mm wide longitudinal striations and micro-structures (irregular ridges) among the striations in a 2010 paper.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hasegawa2010_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hasegawa2010-23">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         23
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sigilmassasaurus">
       <i>
        Sigilmassasaurus
       </i>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">
       Fossils
      </a>
      of this dinosaur were recovered at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kem_Kem_Formation" title="Kem Kem Formation">
       Kem Kem Formation
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Tafilalt" title="Tafilalt">
       Tafilalt
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">
       Oasis
      </a>
      region of
      <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
       Morocco
      </a>
      , near the site of the ancient city of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sijilmassa" title="Sijilmassa">
       Sijilmassa
      </a>
      , for which it was named.
      <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
       Canadian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paleontologist" title="Paleontologist">
       paleontologist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dale_Russell" title="Dale Russell">
       Dale Russell
      </a>
      named
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      in 1996, from the ancient city and the
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      word
      <i>
       sauros
      </i>
      ("lizard"). A single
      <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">
       species
      </a>
      was named,
      <i>
       S. brevicollis
      </i>
      , which is derived from the
      <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
       Latin
      </a>
      <i>
       brevis
      </i>
      ("short") and
      <i>
       collum
      </i>
      ("neck"), because the neck
      <a href="/wiki/Vertebra" title="Vertebra">
       vertebrae
      </a>
      are very short from front to back. Russell also described another specimen as a possible second species of
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      , although he chose not to name it due to its incomplete nature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell96_34-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-russell96-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      comes from red sandstone sediments in southern Morocco, which are known by various names, including the
      <i>
       Grs rouges infracnomaniens
      </i>
      , Continental Red Beds, and lower
      <a href="/wiki/Kem_Kem_Beds" title="Kem Kem Beds">
       Kem Kem Beds
      </a>
      . These rocks date back to the
      <a href="/wiki/Cenomanian" title="Cenomanian">
       Cenomanian
      </a>
      , the earliest
      <a href="/wiki/Stage_(stratigraphy)" title="Stage (stratigraphy)">
       stage
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Late_Cretaceous" title="Late Cretaceous">
       Late Cretaceous
      </a>
      Period, approximately 100 to 94
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Million_years_ago" title="Million years ago">
       million years ago
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal96_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal96-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Holotype" title="Holotype">
       holotype
      </a>
      , or original specimen, of
      <i>
       S. brevicollis
      </i>
      ,
      <b>
       CMN 41857
      </b>
      , is a single posterior
      <a href="/wiki/Neck" title="Neck">
       neck
      </a>
      vertebra, although Russell referred about fifteen other vertebrae found in the same formation to the species. Other material had been found in
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      , and was known as "Spinosaurus B".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stromer34_20-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-stromer34-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Russell considered this Egyptian specimen, IPHG 1922 X45, to belong to
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      or a closely related animal, naming it as a
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      sp. A second
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      sp. was by him based on specimen CMN 41629, an anterior dorsal vertebra. "Spinosaurus B" would be intermediate in build between this latter
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      sp. and
      <i>
       S. brevicollis
      </i>
      . Russell created the family
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sigilmassasauridae" title="Sigilmassasauridae">
       Sigilmassasauridae
      </a>
      for these animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell96_34-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-russell96-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The neck vertebrae of these dinosaurs are wider from side to side, about 50%, than they are long from front to back. Whether the neck as a whole was particularly short, is unknown: the holotype vertebra is a cervicodorsal, from the transition between the neck and the back, which would not be long anyway. The exact position of
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      within the theropod family tree is unknown, but it belongs somewhere inside the theropod subgroup known as
      <a href="/wiki/Tetanurae" title="Tetanurae">
       Tetanurae
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holtzetal04_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-holtzetal04-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some scientists do not believe that
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      is a valid genus. In 1996,
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Sereno" title="Paul Sereno">
       Paul Sereno
      </a>
      and colleagues described a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">
        Carcharodontosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">
       skull
      </a>
      (SGM-Din-1) from Morocco, as well as a neck vertebra (SGM-Din-3) which resembled that of "Spinosaurus B," which they therefore
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synonymy" title="Synonymy">
       synonymized
      </a>
      with
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal96_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal96-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A later study went further, calling
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      itself a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junior_synonym" title="Junior synonym">
       junior synonym
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal98_21-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal98-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      More recently, however, it was revealed that SGM-Din-3, which was used to synonymize
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      and "Spinosaurus B" was not actually associated with SGM-Din-1, the
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      skull described in 1996, and shows clear differences with the holotype of
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      . Other features of "Spinosaurus B" also clearly differ from
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      , lending support to the notion that it (and therefore
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      ) is a separate taxon. The same study claimed that the tail vertebrae by Russell assigned to the species were in fact those of
      <a href="/wiki/Iguanodontia" title="Iguanodontia">
       iguanodonts
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-novasetal05_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-novasetal05-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study in 2013 confirmed that
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      was valid and an indeterminate member of the Tetanurae.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In Ibrahim et al. (2014), the specimens of
      <i>
       Sigilmassasaurus
      </i>
      was referred to
      <i>
       Spinosaurus aegyptiacus
      </i>
      together with "Spinosaurus B" as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neotype" title="Neotype">
       neotype
      </a>
      and
      <i>
       Spinosaurus maroccanus
      </i>
      was considered as a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nomen_dubium" title="Nomen dubium">
        nomen dubium
       </a>
      </i>
      following the conclusions of the other papers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dalsassoetal05_3-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-dalsassoetal05-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ibrahim_et_al_2014_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ibrahim_et_al_2014-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal98_21-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal98-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Paleobiology">
       Paleobiology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Function_of_neural_spines">
       Function of neural spines
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2161" data-file-width="1659" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cb/Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg/170px-Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cb/Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg/255px-Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cb/Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg/340px-Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_vertebrae.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        1915 llustration of
        <i>
         S. aegyptiacus
        </i>
        dorsal vertebrae
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The function of the dinosaur's sail or hump is uncertain; scientists have proposed several
      <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">
       hypotheses
      </a>
      including heat regulation and display. In addition, such a prominent feature on its back could make it appear even larger than it was, intimidating other animals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The structure may have been used for
      <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">
       thermoregulation
      </a>
      . If the structure contained abundant blood vessels, the animal could have used the sail's large surface area to absorb heat. This would imply that the animal was only partly warm-blooded at best and lived in climates where nighttime temperatures were cool or low and the sky usually not cloudy. It is also possible that the structure was used to radiate excess heat from the body, rather than to collect it. Large animals, due to the relatively small ratio of surface area of their body compared to the overall volume (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haldane%27s_principle" title="Haldane's principle">
       Haldane's principle
      </a>
      ), face far greater problems of dissipating excess heat at higher temperatures than gaining it at lower. Sails of large dinosaurs added considerably to the skin area of their bodies, with minimum increase of volume. Furthermore, if the sail was turned away from the sun, or positioned at a 90 degree angle towards a cooling wind, the animal would quite effectively cool itself in the warm climate of Cretaceous Africa.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LBH75_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LBH75-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Bailey (1997) was of the opinion that a sail could have absorbed more heat than it radiated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bailey proposed instead that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      and other dinosaurs with long neural spines had fatty humps on their backs for energy storage, insulation, and shielding from heat.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Elaborate body structures of many modern-day animals usually serve to attract members of the opposite sex during mating. It is quite possible that the sails or humps of these dinosaurs were used for courtship, in a way similar to a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peacock" title="Peacock">
       peacock
      </a>
      's tail. Stromer speculated that males and females may have differed in the size of the neural spine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer15_13-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer15-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Finally, it is quite possible that the sail or hump combined these functions, acting normally as a heat regulator, becoming a courting aid during the mating season, being used to cool itself and, on occasions, turning into an intimidating device when an animal was feeling threatened.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">
       Diet
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4272" data-file-width="2848" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg/170px-Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg/255px-Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg/340px-Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_new_skull.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Reconstructed skull and neck
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      It is unclear whether
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was primarily a terrestrial predator or a
      <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">
       piscivore
      </a>
      , as indicated by its elongated jaws, conical teeth and raised nostrils. The hypothesis of spinosaurs as specialized fish eaters has been suggested before by A. J. Charig and A. C. Milner for
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Baryonyx" title="Baryonyx">
        Baryonyx
       </a>
      </i>
      . They base this on the anatomical similarity with crocodilians and the presence of digestive acid-etched fish scales in the rib cage of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_specimen" title="Type specimen">
       type specimen
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM97_40-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-CM97-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Large fish are known from the faunas containing other spinosaurids, including the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mawsonia_(fish)" title="Mawsonia (fish)">
        Mawsonia
       </a>
      </i>
      , in the mid-Cretaceous of northern Africa and Brazil. Direct evidence for spinosaur diet comes from related
      <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">
       European
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
       South American
      </a>
      taxa.
      <i>
       Baryonyx
      </i>
      was found with fish scales and bones from juvenile
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Iguanodon" title="Iguanodon">
        Iguanodon
       </a>
      </i>
      in its stomach, while a tooth embedded in a South American
      <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">
       pterosaur
      </a>
      bone suggests that spinosaurs occasionally preyed on pterosaurs,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was likely to have been a generalized and opportunistic predator, possibly a Cretaceous equivalent of large
      <a href="/wiki/Grizzly_bear" title="Grizzly bear">
       grizzly bears
      </a>
      , being biased toward fishing, though it undoubtedly
      <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">
       scavenged
      </a>
      and took many kinds of small or medium-sized prey.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GSP88_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GSP88-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A study by Cuff and Rayfield (2013) concluded that bio-mechanical data suggests that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was not an
      <a href="/wiki/Obligate" title="Obligate">
       obligate
      </a>
      piscivore and that its diet was more closely associated with each individual's size.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cuff13_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-cuff13-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The characteristic
      <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Other_directional_terms" title="Anatomical terms of location">
       rostral
      </a>
      morphology of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      allowed its jaws to resist bending in the vertical direction, however its jaws were poorly adapted with respect to resisting lateral bending.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cuff13_14-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-cuff13-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1191" data-file-width="1955" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG/220px-Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG/330px-Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG/440px-Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_sp_Marocco_Cenom.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Tooth from Morocco
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 2009, Dal Sasso
      <i>
       et al
      </i>
      . reported the results of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_computed_tomography" title="X-ray computed tomography">
       X-ray computed tomography
      </a>
      of the MSNM V4047 snout.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalSasso2009_42-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DalSasso2009-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the
      <a href="/wiki/Foramen" title="Foramen">
       foramina
      </a>
      on the outside all communicated with a space on the inside of the snout, the authors speculated that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      had
      <a href="/wiki/Mechanoreceptor" title="Mechanoreceptor">
       pressure receptors
      </a>
      inside the space that allowed it to hold its snout at the surface of the water to detect swimming prey species without seeing them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DalSasso2009_42-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DalSasso2009-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A 2010
      <a href="/wiki/Isotope_analysis" title="Isotope analysis">
       isotope analysis
      </a>
      by Romain Amiot and colleagues found that
      <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_oxygen" title="Isotopes of oxygen">
       oxygen isotope
      </a>
      ratios of spinosaurid teeth, including teeth of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      , indicate semiaquatic lifestyles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMetal10_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RMetal10-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Isotope ratios from tooth enamel and from other parts of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      (found in Morocco and Tunisia) and of other predators from the same area such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">
        Carcharodontosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      were compared with isotopic compositions from contemporaneous theropods, turtles, and crocodilians.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMetal10_43-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-RMetal10-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The study found that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      teeth from five of six sampled localities had oxygen isotope ratios closer to those of turtles and crocodilians when compared with other theropod teeth from the same localities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMetal10_43-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-RMetal10-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The authors postulated that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      switched between terrestrial and aquatic habitats to compete for food with large crocodilians and other large theropods respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RMetal10_43-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-RMetal10-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Posture">
       Posture
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg">
          <img alt="Old hip region" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg/200px-Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg/300px-Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg/400px-Spinosaurus_in_Japan.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Hip region of an older reconstruction
        </div>
       </div>
       <div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
        <div class="thumbimage">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_hip.jpg">
          <img alt="New hip region" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4272" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Spinosaurus_hip.jpg/200px-Spinosaurus_hip.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Spinosaurus_hip.jpg/300px-Spinosaurus_hip.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Spinosaurus_hip.jpg/400px-Spinosaurus_hip.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
         Hip region of the new reconstruction
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although traditionally depicted as a
      <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">
       biped
      </a>
      , it has been suggested since the mid-1970s that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was at least an occasional
      <a href="/wiki/Quadrupedalism" title="Quadrupedalism">
       quadruped
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG82_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DFG82-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LBH75_39-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LBH75-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has been bolstered by the discovery of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Baryonyx" title="Baryonyx">
        Baryonyx
       </a>
      </i>
      , a relative with robust arms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG00_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DFG00-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because of the mass of the hypothesized fatty dorsal humps of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      , Bailey (1997) was open to the possibility of a quadrupedal posture,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBB97_12-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-JBB97-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      leading to new restorations of it as such.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG00_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DFG00-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The hypothesis that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      had a typical quadrupedal gait has fallen out of favor, though spinosaurids may have crouched in a quadrupedal posture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CM97_40-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-CM97-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Theropods, including spinosaurids, could not pronate their hands (rotate the forearm so the palm faced the ground),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carpenter2002_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-carpenter2002-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but a resting position on the side of the hand was possible, as shown by fossil prints from an Early Jurassic theropod.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMetal09_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AMetal09-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2014 paper describing new material of
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      , proposed that its legs were too short for it to move effectively on land. The reconstruction used in the study was an extrapolation based on different sized individuals, scaled to what was assumed to be the correct proportions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spinosaurus_2014_47-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Spinosaurus_2014-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Palaeontologist John Hutchinson of the Royal Veterinary College of the University of London has expressed scepticism to the new reconstruction, and cautioned that using different specimens can result in inaccurate chimaeras.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Scott Hartman also expressed criticism because he believes the legs and the pelvis were inaccurately scaled (27% too short) and don't match the published lengths.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shartman_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-shartman-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, responses from Ibrahim et al. to Mark Witton have been positively received as reliable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Paleoecology">
       Paleoecology
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" style="width:640px;height:480px" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv/640px--Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" class="kskin" data-durationhint="10.678" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/b/b5/Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv/Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm;         <img alt="File:Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv/220px-seek%3D0-Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/>
        <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b5/Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv" target="new" title="Play media">
         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Feeding-Mechanics-in-Spinosaurid-Theropods-and-Extant-Crocodilians-pone.0065295.s007.ogv" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        CT scan of partial snout NHMUK 16665, used in a biomechanical study
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The environment inhabited by
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      is only partially understood, and covers a great deal of what is now northern Africa. The region of Africa
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      is preserved in dates from 112
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      to 97 million years ago.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith06_17-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Smith06-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 1996 study concluded from Moroccan fossils that
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Carcharodontosaurus" title="Carcharodontosaurus">
        Carcharodontosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Deltadromeus" title="Deltadromeus">
        Deltadromeus
       </a>
      </i>
      "ranged across north Africa during the late Cretaceous (Cenomanian)."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-serenoetal96_35-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-serenoetal96-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Those
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      that lived in the Bahariya Formation of what is now
      <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">
       Egypt
      </a>
      may have contended with shoreline conditions on
      <a href="/wiki/Mudflat" title="Mudflat">
       tidal flats
      </a>
      and channels, living in
      <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">
       mangrove
      </a>
      forests alongside similarly large dinosaurian predators
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bahariasaurus" title="Bahariasaurus">
        Bahariasaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Carcharodontosaurus
      </i>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Titanosaur" title="Titanosaur">
       titanosaur
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauropods" title="Sauropods">
       sauropods
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Paralititan" title="Paralititan">
        Paralititan
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Aegyptosaurus" title="Aegyptosaurus">
        Aegyptosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Crocodylomorpha" title="Crocodylomorpha">
       crocodylomorphs
      </a>
      , bony and cartilaginous fish, turtles, lizards, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plesiosaur" title="Plesiosaur">
       plesiosaurs
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JSetal01_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JSetal01-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the dry season it might have resorted to preying on
      <a href="/wiki/Pterosaur" title="Pterosaur">
       pterosaurs
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naish.2C_2012_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-naish.2C_2012-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This situation resembles that in the Late
      <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">
       Jurassic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Morrison_Formation" title="Morrison Formation">
       Morrison Formation
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">
       North America
      </a>
      , which boasts up to five theropod genera over one tonne in weight, as well as several smaller genera (Henderson, 1998; Holtz
      <i>
       et al.
      </i>
      , 2004). Differences in head shape and body size among the large North African theropods may have been enough to allow
      <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">
       niche
      </a>
      partitioning as seen among the many different predator species found today in the
      <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
       African
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">
       savanna
      </a>
      (Farlow &amp; Pianka, 2002).
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">
       In popular culture
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1613" data-file-width="2358" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg/220px-Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg/330px-Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg/440px-Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinosaurus_Tierpark_Germendorf.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Sculpture in Germany, reflecting how the animal was depicted prior to 2014
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      appeared in the 2001 film
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Jurassic_Park_III" title="Jurassic Park III">
        Jurassic Park III
       </a>
      </i>
      , replacing
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">
        Tyrannosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      as the main antagonist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The film's consulting paleontologist
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_R._Horner" title="John R. Horner">
       John R. Horner
      </a>
      was quoted as saying: "If we base the ferocious factor on the length of the animal, there was nothing that ever lived on this planet that could match this creature [
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      ]. Also my hypothesis is that T-rex was actually a scavenger rather than a killer.
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was really the predatory animal."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (He has since retracted the statement about T. Rex being a scavenger.) In the film,
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      was portrayed as larger and more powerful than
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tyrannosaurus" title="Tyrannosaurus">
        Tyrannosaurus
       </a>
      </i>
      : in a scene depicting a battle between the two resurrected predators,
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      emerges victorious by snapping the tyrannosaur's neck.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      has long been depicted in popular books about dinosaurs, although only recently has there been enough information about spinosaurids for an accurate depiction. After an influential 1955 skeletal reconstruction by Lapparent and Lavocat
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lapparent_57-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lapparent-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      based on a 1936 diagram by Stromer,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stromer36_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Stromer36-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      it has been treated as a generalized upright theropod, with a skull similar to that of other large theropods and a sail on its back, even having four-fingered hands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DFG00_44-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-DFG00-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to films, action figures, video games, and books,
      <i>
       Spinosaurus
      </i>
      has been depicted on postage stamps such as ones from
      <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">
       Angola
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/The_Gambia" title="The Gambia">
       The Gambia
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">
       Tanzania
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

         <a href="#cite_ref-TH07_2-2">
